            function createLink(src) {
                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                        preloadLink.href = src;

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of Eucalyptus grandis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2014 510:7505"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-06-11"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Eucalypts are the world&#8217;s most widely planted hardwood trees. Their outstanding diversity, adaptability and growth have made them a global renewable resource of fibre and energy. We sequenced and assembled &gt;94% of the 640-megabase genome of Eucalyptus grandis. Of 36,376 predicted protein-coding genes, 34% occur in tandem duplications, the largest proportion thus far in plant genomes. Eucalyptus also shows the highest diversity of genes for specialized metabolites such as terpenes that act as chemical defence and provide unique pharmaceutical oils. Genome sequencing of the E. grandis sister species E. globulus and a set of inbred E. grandis tree genomes reveals dynamic genome evolution and hotspots of inbreeding depression. The E. grandis genome is the first reference for the eudicot order Myrtales and is placed here sister to the eurosids. This resource expands our understanding of the unique biology of large woody perennials and provides a powerful tool to accelerate comparative biology, breeding and biotechnology. The Eucalyptus grandis genome has been sequenced, revealing the greatest number of tandem duplications of any plant genome sequenced so far, and the highest diversity of genes for specialized metabolites that act as chemical defence and provide unique pharmaceutical oils; genome sequencing of the sister species E. globulus and a set of inbred E. grandis tree genomes reveals dynamic genome evolution and hotspots of inbreeding depression. Fast-growing Eucalyptus trees form the basis of an international pulp, paper and chemical cellulose industry and they are also seen as potential biomass feedstocks for bioenergy and biomaterials. The genome of Eucalyptus grandis has now been sequenced. It contains the greatest number of tandem duplications so far found in a plant genome, as well as the highest diversity of genes for specialized metabolites that act as chemical defence and provide unique pharmaceutical oils. Comparison with the sister species E. globulus and with other E. grandis lines reveals dynamic genome evolution and hotspots of inbreeding depression. The availability of comprehensive genomic data will be of use in work on accelerating breeding cycles for productivity and wood quality and developing eucalypt strains suited to a variety of habitats."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Eucalypts are the world’s most widely planted hardwood trees. Their outstanding diversity, adaptability and growth have made them a global renewable resource of fibre and energy. We sequenced and assembled &gt;94% of the 640-megabase genome of <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i>. Of 36,376 predicted protein-coding genes, 34% occur in tandem duplications, the largest proportion thus far in plant genomes. <i>Eucalyptus</i> also shows the highest diversity of genes for specialized metabolites such as terpenes that act as chemical defence and provide unique pharmaceutical oils. Genome sequencing of the <i>E. grandis</i> sister species <i>E. globulus</i> and a set of inbred <i>E. grandis</i> tree genomes reveals dynamic genome evolution and hotspots of inbreeding depression. The <i>E. grandis</i> genome is the first reference for the eudicot order Myrtales and is placed here sister to the eurosids. This resource expands our understanding of the unique biology of large woody perennials and provides a powerful tool to accelerate comparative biology, breeding and biotechnology.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>A major opportunity for a sustainable energy and biomaterials economy in many parts of the world lies in a better understanding of the molecular basis of superior growth and adaptation in woody plants. Part of this opportunity involves species of <i>Eucalyptus</i> L’Hér, a genus of woody perennials native to Australia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Byrne, M. Phylogeny, diversity and evolution of eucalypts. in Plant Genome: Biodiversity and Evolution, Part E: Phanerogams-Angiosperm Vol. 1 (eds Sharma, A. K. &amp; Sharma, A. ) 303–346 (Science Publishers, 2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1857">1</a></sup>. The remarkable adaptability of eucalypts coupled with their fast growth and superior wood properties has driven their rapid adoption for plantation forestry in more than 100 countries across six continents (&gt;20 million ha)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Iglesias, I. &amp; Wiltermann, D. in Eucalyptologics Information Resources on Eucalypt Cultivation Worldwide 
                    http://www.git-forestry.com
                    
                   (GIT Forestry Consulting, retrieved, 29 March 2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1861">2</a></sup>, making eucalypts the most widely planted hardwood forest trees in the world. The subtropical <i>E. grandis</i> and the temperate <i>E. globulus</i> stand out as targets of breeding programmes worldwide. Planted eucalypts provide key renewable resources for the production of pulp, paper, biomaterials and bioenergy, while mitigating human pressures on native forests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Bauhus, J., van der Meer, P. J. &amp; Kanninen, M. Ecosystem Goods and Services from Plantation Forests 254 (Earthscan, 2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1872">3</a></sup>. Eucalypts also have a large diversity and high concentration of essential oils (mixtures of mono- and sesquiterpenes), many of which have ecological functions as well as medicinal and industrial uses. Predominantly outcrossers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Byrne, M. Phylogeny, diversity and evolution of eucalypts. in Plant Genome: Biodiversity and Evolution, Part E: Phanerogams-Angiosperm Vol. 1 (eds Sharma, A. K. &amp; Sharma, A. ) 303–346 (Science Publishers, 2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1876">1</a></sup> with hermaphroditic animal-pollinated flowers, eucalypts are highly heterozygous and display pre- and postzygotic barriers to selfing to reduce inbreeding depression for fitness and survival<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Costa e Silva, J., Hardner, C., Tilyard, P. &amp; Potts, B. M. The effects of age and environment on the expression of inbreeding depression in Eucalyptus globulus. Heredity 107, 50–60 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1880">4</a></sup>.</p><p>To mitigate the challenge of assembling a highly heterozygous genome, we sequenced the genome of ‘BRASUZ1’, a 17-year-old <i>E. grandis</i> genotype derived from one generation of selfing. The availability of annotated forest tree genomes from two separately evolving rosid lineages, <i>Eucalyptus</i> (order Myrtales) and <i>Populus</i> (order Malpighiales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1896">5</a></sup>), in combination with genomes from domesticated woody plants (for example, <i>Vitis</i>, <i>Prunus</i>, <i>Citrus</i>), provides a comparative foundation for addressing fundamental evolutionary questions related to the biology of woody perennials. Moreover, the unique palaeogeographic evolution of <i>Eucalyptus</i>, that is, isolation from other members of the rosid clade, enables disentangling of the events that led to the modern members of the rosids by characterizing shared and unique whole-genome duplication events and syntenic gene space with other sequenced genomes. The draft genome of <i>E. grandis</i> suggests that the <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome has been shaped by an early lineage-specific genome duplication event and a subsequent high rate of tandem gene duplication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Sequencing, assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Sequencing, assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We assembled a non-redundant chromosome-scale reference (V1.0) sequence for BRASUZ1 based on 6.7× whole-genome Sanger shotgun coverage, paired bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC)-end sequencing and a high-density genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kullan, A. R. K. et al. High-density genetic linkage maps with over 2,400 sequence-anchored DArT markers for genetic dissection in an F2 pseudo-backcross of Eucalyptus grandis × E.  urophylla. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 163–175 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1927">6</a></sup> (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 1). An estimated 94% of the genome is organized into 11 pseudomolecules (605 megabases (Mb), <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Anchoring the genome assembly to an independent linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Petroli, C. D. et al. Genomic characterization of DArT markers based on high-density linkage analysis and physical mapping to the Eucalyptus genome. PLoS ONE 7, e44684 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d16771e1937">7</a></sup> revealed that the remaining 4,941 smaller unanchored scaffolds (totalling 85 Mb) correspond largely to repeat-rich sequences and segments of alternative haplotypes of the assembled chromosomes derived from regions of residual heterozygosity in the otherwise inbred BRASUZ1 genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Eucalyptus grandis genome overview."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> genome overview.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="690"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genome features in 1-Mb intervals across the 11 chromosomes. Units on the circumference show megabase values and chromosomes. <b>a</b>, Gene density (number per Mb, range 6–131). <b>b</b>, Repeat coverage (22–88% per Mb). <b>c</b>, Average expression state (fragments per kilobase of exon per million sequences mapped, FPKM, per gene per Mb, 6–41 per Mb). <b>d</b>, Heterozygosity in inbred siblings (proportion of 28 S<sub>1</sub> offspring heterozygous at position, 0.39–0.93). <b>e</b>, Telomeric repeats. <b>f</b>, Tandem duplication density (2–50). <b>g</b>, <b>h</b>, Single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) identified by resequencing BRASUZ1 in 1-Mb bins (<b>g</b>) and per gene (<b>h</b>, 11,656 genes); homozygous regions (<span class="stix">∼</span>24%) and genes in green and heterozygous regions and genes in purple. Central blue lines connect gene pairs from the most recent whole-genome duplication event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 1</a>).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM54">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>E. grandis</i> genome encodes a large number of predicted protein-coding loci (36,376) of which 89% are expressed in vegetative and reproductive tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>) plus various classes of non-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 2). Of the 36,376 predicted proteins, 30,341 (84%) are included in gene clusters shared with other rosid lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>). Retrotransposons account for the major portion of the genome (44.5%), with long terminal repeat retrotransposons being the most pervasive class (21.9%). DNA transposons encompass only 5.6% of the genome. For this class, Helitron elements are the most abundant with an estimated 15,000 copies or 3.8% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 2).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Genome evolution and phylogeny"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Genome evolution and phylogeny</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>To address the phylogenetic position of <i>Eucalyptus</i>, we performed genome-wide analysis of 17 sequenced plant genomes, generating a matrix of 697,423 aligned amino acid positions from 3,268 orthologue gene clusters (Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3). Studies employing broad taxon sampling but a modest number of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang, H. et al. Rosid radiation and the rapid rise of angiosperm-dominated forests. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3853–3858 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2039">8</a></sup> have consistently recovered two very well-supported clades of eurosids—the fabids and malvids—and grouped <i>Eucalyptus</i> and other Myrtales with the malvids. Our analysis alternatively places <i>Eucalyptus</i> as a sister taxon to the eurosids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 3</a>) and supports the grouping of <i>Populus</i> and <i>Jatropha</i> (order Malpighiales) with malvids rather than fabids, in agreement with other recent whole-genome studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2059">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nature Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2062">10</a></sup>. The discrepancy between our genome-wide analyses and the angiosperm phylogeny group (APG) consensus highlights important methodological trade-offs between sampling more characters (as in our genome-wide study) versus more taxa (as per APG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Martin, W., Deusch, O., Stawski, N., Grunheit, N. &amp; Goremykin, V. Chloroplast genome phylogenetics: why we need independent approaches to plant molecular evolution. Trends Plant Sci. 10, 203–209 (2005)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2066">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jeffroy, O., Brinkmann, H., Delsuc, F. &amp; Philippe, H. Phylogenomics: the beginning of incongruence? Trends Genet. 22, 225–231 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2069">12</a></sup>.</p><p>The evolutionary history of the <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome is marked by a lineage-specific palaeotetraploidy event newly revealed by our genomic analysis, superimposed on the earlier palaeohexaploidy event shared by all eudicots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The whole-genome duplication (WGD) is estimated to have occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>109.9 (105.9–113.9) million years (Myr) ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3 and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>) in a Gondwanan ancestor around the time when Australia and Antarctica began to separate from East Gondwana. This WGD event is considerably older than those typically detected in other rosids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2088">13</a></sup> and could have played a pivotal part in the evolution of the Myrtales lineage and its subsequent diversification from other rosid ancestors. The coincidence of the estimated WGD timing and the origin of the Myrtales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2093">14</a></sup> leads to speculation that the WGD event could be directly related to the origin of the clade. More precise timing will require genomic analysis of other families and genera from the Myrtales.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Eucalyptus grandis genome synteny, duplication pattern and evolutionary history."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> genome synteny, duplication pattern and evolutionary history.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="904"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Paralogous gene pairs in <i>Eucalyptus</i> for the identified palaeohexaploidization (bottom) and palaeotetraploidization (top) events. Each line represents a duplicated gene, and colours reflect origin from the seven ancestral chromosomes (A1, A4, A7, A10, A13, A16, A19). <b>b</b>, Number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) distributions of <i>Eucalyptus</i> paralogues (top) and <i>Eucalyptus</i>–<i>Vitis</i> orthologues (bottom). Blue bars (top) indicate <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values for 378 gene pairs from the palaeotetraploidization WGD event (red dot), and red bars show <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values for 274 gene pairs of the palaeohexaploidization event (red star). <b>c</b>, Evolutionary scenario of genome rearrangements from the Eudicot ancestor to <i>Eucalyptus</i> and other sequenced plant genomes; palaeohistory modified from ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Salse, J. In silico archeogenomics unveils modern plant genome organisation, regulation and evolution. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 122–130 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2148">49</a>.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM55">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM56">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome exhibits substantial conservation of synteny with other rosids as has been demonstrated for the basal rosid lineage represented by <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2180">15</a></sup>. Extending the method previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2184">5</a></sup> we identified 480 pairwise segments of conserved synteny between <i>Eucalyptus</i> and <i>Vitis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3). These segments include 68% of <i>Eucalyptus</i> genes and 76% of <i>Vitis</i> genes used in the analysis. The WGD in the <i>Eucalyptus</i> lineage relative to <i>Vitis</i> is clearly revealed by the 2:1 pattern in which two different <i>Eucalyptus</i> regions are typically collinear with a single region in <i>Vitis</i>. However, the gene content of these segments varies, as more than 95% of the paralogues in <i>Eucalyptus</i> have been lost subsequent to the WGD (a total of 5,896 <i>Vitis</i> genes have 6,158 synteny-confirmed orthologues in <i>Eucalyptus</i>). Half of the total length of the orthologous segments is contributed by segments longer than 1.83 Mb in <i>Eucalyptus</i> and 2.35 Mb in <i>Vitis</i>, suggesting that the loss of redundant genes after the WGD in <i>Eucalyptus</i> was accompanied by a compaction of those parts of the genome.</p><p><i>Eucalyptus</i> chromosome 3, the largest single chromosome in the <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome, is the only chromosome that does not contain inter-chromosomal segmental duplications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), having fused with its WGD homologue. A similar situation occurs in <i>Populus</i> chromosome XVIII. Interestingly, <i>Eucalyptus</i> chromosome 3 and <i>Populus</i> chromosome XVIII nearly exclusively contain the ancestral eudicot chromosome 2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>), despite their independent WGDs. There are no other examples among the currently sequenced dicotyledon genomes that contain a sole single copy of an ancestral chromosome. Moreover, in <i>Eucalyptus</i> and <i>Populus</i>, all other ancestral chromosomes appear to be dispersed and rearranged among the extant chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). The conserved gene content and order (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3) on these chromosomes in two distantly related species could be due to: (1) convergent selection and positional stoichiometry of genes related to long-lived perennial woody habit that favours preservation of certain genes in syntenic order; and/or (2) merged ancestral chromosome structure (that is, multiple telomeres and centromeres on one chromosome) that suppresses gene expression, recombination and/or successive rearrangement. <i>Eucalyptus</i> chromosome 3 has the lowest average gene expression metrics of any of the <i>Eucalyptus</i> chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), favouring the second hypothesis. Alternatively, there are several clusters of shared syntenic genes that appear to be related to perennial habit, including homologues of NAM (no apical meristem, PF02365) and senescence-associated protein (PF02365), several syntenic sets of disease-resistance genes, as well as genes related to cell-wall formation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 2</a>).</p><p>We also find that <i>E. grandis</i> has the largest number of genes in tandem repeats (12,570, 34% of the total) reported among sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3). The low frequency of contig breaks separating tandem gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>) and conserved gene order on independent BAC clones spanning two large tandem gene arrays (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information 1</a>) support the accuracy of the assembly across highly similar tandem copies. Tandem duplication often involves stress-response genes that are retained in a lineage-specific fashion, suggesting that tandem duplication is important for adaptive evolution in dynamically changing environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol. 148, 993–1003 (2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2308">16</a></sup>. For example, more than 80% of the S-domain receptor-like kinase (SDRLK) subfamily occurs in tandem arrays (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 4</a>). There also seems to be a bias in gene retention following tandem duplication in comparison to segmental and whole-genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433–453 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2315">17</a></sup>. Even within the genus <i>Eucalyptus</i>, tandem duplication appears to be dynamic, for example, a cluster of MYB transcription factor genes in <i>E. globulus</i> lacks four of the nine tandem duplicates found in <i>E. grandis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Tandem duplicate statistics for selected plant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature13308/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Despite having the same number of chromosomes (<i>n</i> = 11) and highly co-linear genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hudson, C. J. et al. High synteny and colinearity among Eucalyptus genomes revealed by high-density comparative genetic mapping. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 339–352 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2501">18</a></sup>, eucalypts vary considerably in genome size. <i>E. grandis</i> (640 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Grattapaglia, D. &amp; Bradshaw, H. D. Nuclear DNA content of commercially important Eucalyptus species and hybrids. Can. J. For. Res. 24, 1074–1078 (1994)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2508">19</a></sup>) and <i>E. globulus</i> (530 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Grattapaglia, D. &amp; Bradshaw, H. D. Nuclear DNA content of commercially important Eucalyptus species and hybrids. Can. J. For. Res. 24, 1074–1078 (1994)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2516">19</a></sup>) represent different sections (<i>Latoangulatae</i> and <i>Maidenaria</i>) within the subgenus <i>Symphyomyrtus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Brooker, M. I. H. A new classification of the genus Eucalyptus L'Her. (Myrtaceae). Aust. Syst. Bot. 13, 79–148 (2000)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2528">20</a></sup>, estimated to have diverged in the past 36 million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Crisp, M. D., Burrows, G. E., Cook, L. G., Thornhill, A. H. &amp; Bowman, D. M. Flammable biomes dominated by eucalypts originated at the Cretaceous-Palaeogene boundary. Natuer Commun. 2, 193 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2532">21</a></sup>. Resequencing of the subtropical <i>E. grandis</i> (BRASUZ1) and a representative of the temperate <i>E. globulus</i> (‘X46’, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3) revealed that many small, non-transposable element (TE)-derived changes distributed throughout the genome (164,813 regions; mean length 538 bp, median 230 bp, maximum 30,610 bp, total 88.7 Mb) account for nearly all of the genome size difference between the two species. Recent TE activity accounts for only 2 Mb of the size difference. This is in contrast to other studies in closely related plant species that report a predominant role for TEs in genome size evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ågren, J. A. &amp; Wright, S. I. Co-evolution between transposable elements and their hosts: a major factor in genome size evolution? Chromosome Res. 19, 777–786 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2546">22</a></sup>. Using sequence data from other <i>Eucalyptus</i> species taxonomically positioned around the <i>E. grandis</i>–<i>E. globulus</i> split (J. Tibbits, unpublished data), we estimate that since divergence, <i>E. grandis</i> has gained 58 Mb and lost 12 Mb, while <i>E. globulus</i> has gained 15 Mb and lost 24 Mb, suggesting more active genome size evolution than was apparent from previous estimates.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Genetic load and heterozygosity"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Genetic load and heterozygosity</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Eucalypts are preferentially outcrossing with late-acting post-zygotic self-incompatibility resulting in outcrossing rates that can exceed 90%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Byrne, M. Phylogeny, diversity and evolution of eucalypts. in Plant Genome: Biodiversity and Evolution, Part E: Phanerogams-Angiosperm Vol. 1 (eds Sharma, A. K. &amp; Sharma, A. ) 303–346 (Science Publishers, 2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2574">1</a></sup>, high levels of nucleotide variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Külheim, C., Hui Yeoh, S., Maintz, J., Foley, W. &amp; Moran, G. Comparative SNP diversity among four Eucalyptus species for genes from secondary metabolite biosynthetic pathways. BMC Genomics 10, 452 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2578">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Novaes, E. et al. High-throughput gene and SNP discovery in Eucalyptus grandis, an uncharacterized genome. BMC Genomics 9, 312 (2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2581">24</a></sup> and accumulation of genetic load and expression of inbreeding depression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Costa e Silva, J., Hardner, C., Tilyard, P. &amp; Potts, B. M. The effects of age and environment on the expression of inbreeding depression in Eucalyptus globulus. Heredity 107, 50–60 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2585">4</a></sup>. A microsatellite survey of BRASUZ1 and its inbred siblings indicated putative hotspots of genetic load (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 4). To investigate the distribution of preserved heterozygosity further, we resequenced an unrelated (outbred) <i>E. grandis</i> parental genotype M35D2 and 28 of its S<sub>1</sub> offspring. The offspring were genotyped using 308,784 high-confidence heterozygous sites (within 22,619 genes) identified in M35D2 (Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 4). Contrary to Mendelian expectation of 50% retained heterozygosity after selfing, we observed 52% to 79% heterozygosity in the 28 S<sub>1</sub> offspring (average of 66%). In all chromosomes except 5 and 11, heterozygosity was high (&gt;80%) in long chromosome segments with peaks at &gt;90% on chromosomes 6, 7 and 9 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). Despite the strong bias towards heterozygosity in these regions, a small proportion of either homozygous haplotype was always present, suggesting that there are genetic backgrounds in which homozygosity of any particular gene is not lethal. One exception is on chromosome 4, where a 25-Mb region is completely devoid of one homozygous class across all surveyed genotypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 4).</p><p>The genetic architecture of genetic load and contribution of individual loci to inbreeding depression are largely unknown for woody perennials and present a barrier to rapid domestication via recurrent inbred mating. Our results suggest that a model of genome-wide cumulative effects of many small recessive alleles affecting overall fitness and survival best explains the architecture of inbreeding depression in <i>Eucalyptus</i>. This result is consistent with recent genome-wide selection experiments in <i>Eucalyptus</i> showing that a multifactorial model of a few hundred small effects throughout the genome contribute additively to height growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Resende, M. D. et al. Genomic selection for growth and wood quality in Eucalyptus: capturing the missing heritability and accelerating breeding for complex traits in forest trees. New Phytol. 194, 116–128 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2621">25</a></sup>, in contrast to earlier suggestions of the existence of a relatively small number of loci of larger effect as reported in several biparental QTL mapping studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Grattapaglia, D. et al. Progress in Myrtaceae genetics and genomics: Eucalyptus as the pivotal genus. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 463–508 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2625">26</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Lignocellulosic biomass production"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Lignocellulosic biomass production</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>Whereas woody growth habit (the ability to produce radial secondary tissues from a vascular cambium) is polyphyletic, having appeared and disappeared multiple times across more than 30 diverse taxa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Groover, A. T. What genes make a tree a tree? Trends Plant Sci. 10, 210–214 (2005)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2637">27</a></sup>, secondary cell wall formation itself is highly conserved across vascular plants. Large woody plants produce secondary cell walls on a vastly different scale from that of herbaceous plants. Approximately 80% of woody biomass comprises cellulose and hemicellulose, with the remaining biomass primarily composed of lignin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Boerjan, W., Ralph, J. &amp; Baucher, M. Lignin biosynthesis. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 54, 519–546 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2641">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Mizrachi, E., Mansfield, S. D. &amp; Myburg, A. A. Cellulose factories: advancing bioenergy production from forest trees. New Phytol. 194, 54–62 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2644">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Scheller, H. V. &amp; Ulvskov, P. Hemicelluloses. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 61, 263–289 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2647">30</a></sup>. A major determinant of industrial processing efficiency lies in secondary cell wall ultrastructure, which is dependent on interactions among these biopolymers. We identified putative functional homologues of genes encoding 18 enzymatic steps of cellulose and heteroxylan biosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 5). Despite the lineage-specific WGD event and the high number of genes in tandem duplications, relative and absolute expression levels (See Methods) suggest that most of the key enzymatic steps involve only one or two functional homologues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>), which are highly and specifically expressed in xylem tissue. The xylem expression pattern of genes involved in sucrose catabolism suggests that <i>Eucalyptus</i> uses both direct (SUSY) and indirect (INV) pathways for the production of UDP-glucose (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Notably, the two <i>sucrose synthase 4</i> homologues (Eucgr.C03199 and Eucgr.C00769) are expressed at high levels in xylem tissue and account for 70% and 18% of total sucrose synthase expression, respectively. These genes, found on chromosome 3 in <i>Eucalyptus</i>, are part of a syntenic set of genes found on <i>Populus</i> chromosome XVIII, indicating that these genes pre-date the speciation events that separate these genera. There are 10 multigene families encoding phenylpropanoid biosynthesis genes that have expanded, mostly through tandem duplication, to include 174 genes in <i>E. grandis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 5). Phylogenetic analysis and expression profiling have allowed us to define a core set of 24 genes, as well as five novel lignification candidates, preferentially and highly expressed in developing xylem (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 5). These results highlight the central role of tandem gene duplication in shaping functional diversity in <i>Eucalyptus</i> and suggest that subfunctionalization within these expanded gene families has prioritized specific genes for wood formation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genes involved in cellulose and xylan biosynthesis in wood-forming tissues of Eucalyptus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Genes involved in cellulose and xylan biosynthesis in wood-forming tissues of <i>Eucalyptus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="615"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Relative (yellow–blue scale) and absolute (white–red scale) expression profiles of secondary cell-wall-related genes implicated in cellulose and xylan biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Mizrachi, E., Mansfield, S. D. &amp; Myburg, A. A. Cellulose factories: advancing bioenergy production from forest trees. New Phytol. 194, 54–62 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2705">29</a></sup>. Sugar and polymer intermediates are shown in green, while the proteins (enzymes) involved in each step are shown in blue. Detailed protein names, annotation and mRNA-seq expression data are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 5</a>. ST, shoot tips; YL, young leaves; ML, mature leaves; FL, floral buds; RT, roots; PH, phloem, IX, immature xylem. Absolute expression level (FPKM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2712">50</a></sup>) is only shown for immature xylem, the target secondary cell-wall-producing tissue. DUF, domain of unknown function; GATL, galacturonosyl transferase-like; GUX, glucuronic acid substitution of xylan; HEX, hexokinase; INV, invertase; IRX, irregular xylem; PGM, phosphoglucomutase; SUSY, sucrose synthase; RWA, reduced wall acetylation; UGD, UDP-glucose dehydrogenase; UGP, UDP-glucose pyrophosphorylase; UXS, UDP-xylose synthase.</p><p>
