                      </p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM58">Source data</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Secondary metabolites and oils"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Secondary metabolites and oils</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>It is generally thought that the extremely diverse array of secondary metabolites observed within <i>Eucalyptus</i> defends against a comparably diverse array of biotic pests, pathogens and herbivores encountered across its natural range. Many of the defence compounds are terpenoid based, including the commercially valuable eucalyptus oil, which is composed largely of 1,8-cineole. The conjugation of terpenes with phloroglucinol derivatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Eschler, B. M., Pass, D. M., Willis, R. &amp; Foley, W. J. Distribution of foliar formylated phloroglucinol derivatives amongst Eucalyptus species. Biochem. Syst. Ecol. 28, 813–824 (2000)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2748">31</a></sup>, as well as the formation of monoterpene glucose esters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Goodger, J. Q. &amp; Woodrow, I. E. α,β-Unsaturated monoterpene acid glucose esters: structural diversity, bioactivities and functional roles. Phytochemistry 72, 2259–2266 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2752">32</a></sup>, leads to the myriad of defence compounds that vary across the genus. <i>E. grandis</i> has the largest observed number of terpene synthase genes among all sequenced plant genomes (<i>n</i> = 113 compared to a range of <i>n</i> = 2 in <i>Physcomitrella</i> to 83 in <i>Vitis</i>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), as well as a marked expansion of several phenylpropanoid gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 5). Furthermore, a subgroup of R2R3-MYB transcription factor genes known to be involved in the regulation of the phenylpropanoid pathway in <i>Arabidopsis</i> is expanded by tandem duplication in <i>Eucalyptus</i> to yield 16 genes with diverse expression profiles (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9</a>) possibly associated with the wide range of phenylpropanoid-derived compounds found in <i>Eucalyptus.</i></p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Interspecific phylogenetic analysis and classification of terpene synthase (TPS) genes from Eucalyptus grandis and other sequenced plant genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Interspecific phylogenetic analysis and classification of terpene synthase (TPS) genes from <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> and other sequenced plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="363"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree shows all TPS genes found in eight plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 6</a>). TPS subfamilies are indicated on the circumference of the circle. The tree has been rooted between the two major groups of type I and type III TPS. The table shows the number of TPS genes from several species obtained from a Pfam search for the two Pfam motifs (PF01397 and PF03936) found in the TPS genes. Colour coding in the table corresponds to that in the tree. The scale bar (0.3) shows the number of amino acid substitutions per site.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM59">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Reproductive biology"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Reproductive biology</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The genus <i>Eucalyptus</i> is named for its unusual floral structure derived from the Greek <i>eu</i>-, well, and <i>kaluptos</i>, covered, which refers to the operculum that covers the floral buds before anthesis. The ability to produce large amounts of pollen and seed over long generation times increases the reproductive success of woody perennials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Petit, R. J. &amp; Hampe, A. Some evolutionary consequences of being a tree. Annu. Rev. Ecol. Evol. Syst. 37, 187–214 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2840">33</a></sup> and impacts on adaptation and population genetics. Interestingly, the evolution and genetic control of the unique floral structure in <i>Eucalyptus</i> may be reflected in the expansion and deletion of genes typically associated with floral structure (for example, the APETALA1/FRUITFUL-like clade, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 7). <i>SOC1</i>, a type II MADS-box gene that integrates multiple signals related to initiation of flowering, including long days, vernalization and pathways related to gibberellin signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lee, J. &amp; Lee, I. Regulation and function of SOC1, a flowering pathway integrator. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 2247–2254 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2854">34</a></sup>, has been markedly expanded in <i>E. grandis</i> compared to other angiosperms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#Fig14">Extended Data Fig. 10</a>). <i>Eucalyptus</i> is a diverse genus of over 700 species distributed in a wide range of environments ranging from tropical, subtropical and temperate forests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Byrne, M. Phylogeny, diversity and evolution of eucalypts. in Plant Genome: Biodiversity and Evolution, Part E: Phanerogams-Angiosperm Vol. 1 (eds Sharma, A. K. &amp; Sharma, A. ) 303–346 (Science Publishers, 2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2868">1</a></sup>. This environmental heterogeneity encompasses extensive variation in the onset, season and intensity of flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="House, S. M. Reproductive biology of eucalypts. in Eucalypt Ecology: Individuals to Ecosystems (ed. Woinarski, J. ) 30–56 (Cambridge Univ. Press, 1997)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2872">35</a></sup>. Because of <i>SOC1</i>’s diverse roles in environmental control of flowering, the expansion and subfunctionalization of the <i>SOC1</i> subfamily may have contributed to the evolutionary diversification of <i>Eucalyptus</i> by integrating multiple signals into flowering responses relevant to different geographical zones. <i>Eucalyptus</i> may thus provide a model for the evolution of responses to divergent sets of flowering cues required for wide colonization and speciation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions and future directions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions and future directions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The availability of a high-quality reference represents a timely step forward in fundamental studies of adaptation across the diversity of habitats occupied by eucalypt species. The unique biology and evolutionary history of <i>Eucalyptus</i> are reflected in its genome, for example, the expansion of terpene synthesis genes and the large number of tandem repeats, respectively. The coincidence of a lineage-specific WGD with the origin of the Myrtales reinforces the proposed role of genome duplication in angiosperm evolution and underscores the value of additional genome sequencing of families and genera in this important rosid lineage. Future studies of variation in functional genes will provide insights into the relative influences of drift and selection on <i>Eucalyptus</i> evolution and identify mechanisms of speciation and adaptive divergence. Such insight will lead to improved understanding of the response of eucalypts to environmental change. Comparative analysis of the <i>E. grandis</i> genome with those of other large perennials will add crucial insights into the evolutionary innovations that have made eucalypts keystone species that shape biodiversity in diverse ecosystems. The prospect of accelerating breeding cycles for productivity and wood quality via genomic prediction of complex traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Resende, M. D. et al. Genomic selection for growth and wood quality in Eucalyptus: capturing the missing heritability and accelerating breeding for complex traits in forest trees. New Phytol. 194, 116–128 (2012)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2905">25</a></sup> and association genetics is enhanced by the release of the <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome. Genome-enabled derivation of an integrative data framework based on large-scale genotypic and phenotypic data sets will offer increasingly valuable insights into the complex connections between individual genomic elements and the extraordinary phenotypic variation in <i>Eucalyptus</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We used whole-genome shotgun sequencing (6.73× final sequence coverage from 7.7 million Sanger reads) followed by assembly in Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jaffe, D. B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2923">36</a>) and high-density genetic linkage mapping to produce chromosome-scale pseudomolecule sequences of the 11 nuclear chromosomes of BRASUZ1. Protein-coding loci were identified using homology-based FgenesH and GenomeScan predictions and <span class="stix">∼</span>260,000 PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2927">37</a></sup> EST assemblies from <i>E. grandis</i> and sister species. Gene family clustering was performed with the Inparanoid algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Remm, M., Storm, C. E. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in-paralogs from pairwise species comparisons. J. Mol. Biol. 314, 1041–1052 (2001)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2934">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196–D203 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2937">39</a></sup> and peptide sequences analysed with Interproscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–D215 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2941">40</a></sup>, SignalP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Bendtsen, J. D., Nielsen, H., von Heijne, G. &amp; Brunak, S. Improved prediction of signal peptides: SignalP 3.0. J. Mol. Biol. 340, 783–795 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2946">41</a></sup>, Predotar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Small, I., Peeters, N., Legeai, F. &amp; Lurin, C. Predotar: A tool for rapidly screening proteomes for N-terminal targeting sequences. Proteomics 4, 1581–1590 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2950">42</a></sup>, TMHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sonnhammer, E. L., von Heijne, G. &amp; Krogh, A. A hidden Markov model for predicting transmembrane helices in protein sequences. Proc. Int. Conf. Intell. Syst. Mol. Biol. 6, 175–182 (1998)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2954">43</a></sup> and orthology-based projections from <i>Arabidopsis.</i> We performed maximum-likelihood-based phylogenetic reconstruction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2961">44</a></sup> of the green plant phylogeny based on 174,020 peptides encoded by single copy orthologous genes from 17 plant genomes. Protein domains and domain arrangements were analysed to identify a core set of domains and arrangements present in rosid lineages represented by <i>Eucalyptus</i>, <i>Vitis</i>, <i>Populus</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Genome-wide gene expression profiling was performed using Illumina RNA-seq analysis of seven developing tissues from <i>E. grandis.</i> We identified whole-genome duplications using an approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salse, J., Abrouk, M., Murat, F., Quraishi, U. M. &amp; Feuillet, C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinform. 10, 619–630 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2981">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2984">46</a></sup> based on paralogue- and orthologue-specific comparisons of the 36,376 predicted protein-coding genes and further refined the estimated age of the lineage-specific WGD event using a phylogenetic dating approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Fawcett, J. A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2988">13</a></sup>. Genome synteny between <i>E. grandis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was evaluated using the VISTA pipeline infrastructure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Frazer, K. A., Pachter, L., Poliakov, A., Rubin, E. M. &amp; Dubchak, I. VISTA: computational tools for comparative genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W273–W279 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d16771e2999">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Dubchak, I., Poliakov, A., Kislyuk, A. &amp; Brudno, M. Multiple whole-genome alignments without a reference organism. Genome Res. 19, 682–689 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3002">48</a></sup>. Genome resequencing of <i>E. grandis</i> (BRASUZ1) and sister species <i>E. globulus</i> (X46) genomes was performed with Illumina PE100 DNA sequencing. Lignin, cellulose, xylan, terpene and flowering-related gene families were analysed using a combination of gene annotation, phylogenetic analysis and mRNA-seq expression profiling.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Online Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Online Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Whole-genome shotgun sequencing and assembly</h3><p>All sequencing reads were collected with standard Sanger sequencing protocols on ABI 3730XL automated sequencers at the Joint Genome Institute, Walnut Creek, CA. Three different sized libraries were used for the plasmid subclone sequencing process and paired-end sequencing. A total of 3,446,208 reads from the 2.6-kb sized libraries, 3,479,232 reads from the 6.0-kb sized libraries and 518,016 reads from a 36.2–40.6-kb library were sequenced. Two BAC libraries (EG_Ba, 127.5-kb insert and EG_Bb, 155.0-kb insert) were end sequenced to add an additional 294,912 reads for long-range linking.</p><p>The sequence reads were assembled using a modified version of Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jaffe, D. B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3026">36</a>) with parameters maxcliq1 = 100, correct1_passes = 0, n_haplotypes = 2 and BINGE_AND_PURGE = True. The resulting output was then passed through Rebuilder and SquashOverlaps with parameters to merge adjacent assembled alternative haplotypes and subsequently run through another complete Arachne assembly process to finalize the assembly. This produced 6,043 scaffold sequences, with a scaffold L50 of 4.9 Mb and total scaffold size of 692.7 Mb. Scaffolds were screened against bacterial proteins, organelle sequences, GenBank nr and were removed if found to be a contaminant. Additional scaffolds were removed if they (1) consisted of &gt;95% of base pairs that occurred as 24mers four other times in the scaffolds larger than 50 kb; (2) contained a majority of unanchored RNA sequences; or (3) were less than 1 kb in length.</p><p>For chromosome-scale pseudomolecule construction, markers from the genetic map were placed using two methods. SSR-based markers were placed using three successive rounds of e-PCR with <i>N</i> = 0, <i>N</i> = 1 and <i>N</i> = 3. Markers that had sequence associated with them, including SNP markers, were placed with BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3042">51</a></sup> and blastn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3046">52</a></sup>. A total of 19 breaks (16 in high coverage (&gt;6x), 3 in low coverage (≤6x)) were made in scaffolds based on linkage group discontiguity; a subset of the broken scaffolds were combined using 257 joins to form the 11 pseudomolecule chromosomes. Map joins were denoted with 10,000 repeats of the letter N (Ns). The pseudomolecules contained 605.9 Mb out of 691.3 Mb (88%) of the assembled sequence. The final assembly contains 4,952 scaffolds with a contig L50 of 67.2 kb and a scaffold L50 of 53.9 Mb. The completeness of the resulting assembly was estimated using 1,007,962 ESTs from BRASUZ1. The goal of this analysis was to obtain an estimate of the completeness of the assembly, rather than to do a comprehensive examination of gene space. Briefly, ESTs &lt;300 bp were removed, along with chloroplast, mitochondrial or rDNA ESTs. All duplicate ESTs were placed against the genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3051">51</a></sup>. The remaining ESTs were screened for alignments that had ≥90% identity and ≥85% EST coverage. The screened alignments indicated that 98.98% of available expressed gene loci were included in the 11 chromosome assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene prediction</h3><p>To produce the current gene set, we used the homology-based FgenesH and GenomeScan predictions. The best gene prediction at each locus was selected and integrated with EST assemblies using the PASA program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3063">37</a></sup>. The gene set shown in the browser was generated from the input gene models at JGI. The gene prediction pipeline was structured as follows: peptides from diverse angiosperms and <span class="stix">∼</span>260,000 EST assemblies (from <span class="stix">∼</span>2.9 M filtered <i>E. grandis</i> ESTs and <span class="stix">∼</span>2.4 M EST sequences from other closely related (‘sister’) <i>Eucalyptus</i> species, assembled with PASA) were aligned to the genome and their overlaps used to define putative protein-coding gene loci. The corresponding genomic regions were extended by 1 kb in each direction and submitted to FgenesH and GenomeScan, along with related angiosperm peptides and/or ORFs from the overlapping EST assemblies. These two sets of predictions were integrated with expressed sequence information using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3073">37</a></sup> against <span class="stix">∼</span>260,000 <i>Eucalyptus</i> EST assemblies. The results were filtered to remove genes identified as transposon-related.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene family cluster and gene ontology analysis</h3><p>The Inparanoid algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Remm, M., Storm, C. E. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. Automatic clustering of orthologs and in-paralogs from pairwise species comparisons. J. Mol. Biol. 314, 1041–1052 (2001)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3088">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196–D203 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3091">39</a></sup> was used to identify orthologous and paralogous genes that arose through duplication events. Clusters were determined using a reciprocal best pair match and then an algorithm for adding in-paralogues was applied. The peptide sequences used were from <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Caenorhabditis elegans</i>, <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>, <i>Danio rerio</i>, <i>Ectocarpus siliculosus</i>, <i>Escherichia coli</i>, <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, <i>Glycine max</i>, <i>Homo sapiens</i>, <i>Jatropha curcas</i>, <i>Mus musculus</i>, <i>Neurospora crassa</i>, <i>Nostoc punctiforme</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Physcomitrella patens</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i>, <i>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</i>, <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Synechocystis pcc6803</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Zea mays.</i> The sequences were downloaded from Gramene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Youens-Clark, K. et al. Gramene database in 2010: updates and extensions. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D1085–D1094 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3187">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Jaiswal, P. Gramene database: a hub for comparative plant genomics. Methods Mol. Biol. 678, 247–275 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3190">54</a></sup>, Phytozome (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net">http://www.phytozome.net</a>) and Ensembl (<a href="http://www.ensembl.org">http://www.ensembl.org</a>). A functional annotation pipeline similar to the one used for strawberry genome annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nature Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3208">10</a></sup> was used to infer Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nature Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3212">55</a></sup> assignments to 29,841 protein coding genes (<span class="stix">∼</span>82%). Peptide sequences were analysed through an integrated approach involving Interproscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–D215 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3217">40</a></sup>, SignalP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Bendtsen, J. D., Nielsen, H., von Heijne, G. &amp; Brunak, S. Improved prediction of signal peptides: SignalP 3.0. J. Mol. Biol. 340, 783–795 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3221">41</a></sup>, Predotar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Small, I., Peeters, N., Legeai, F. &amp; Lurin, C. Predotar: A tool for rapidly screening proteomes for N-terminal targeting sequences. Proteomics 4, 1581–1590 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3225">42</a></sup>, TMHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sonnhammer, E. L., von Heijne, G. &amp; Krogh, A. A hidden Markov model for predicting transmembrane helices in protein sequences. Proc. Int. Conf. Intell. Syst. Mol. Biol. 6, 175–182 (1998)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3229">43</a></sup> and orthology-based projections from <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Green plant phylogeny</h3><p>We used an integrated approach of gene orthology clustering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nature Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3244">10</a></sup> and an automated workflow for phylogenomic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Robbertse, B., Yoder, R. J., Boyd, A., Reeves, J. &amp; Spatafora, J. W. Hal: an automated pipeline for phylogenetic analyses of genomic data. PLoS Curr. 3, RRN1213 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3248">56</a></sup> to reconstruct land plant phylogeny of peptide sequences. A total of 174,020 peptides encoded by single-copy protein coding orthologous nuclear genes from 17 plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 7</a>) were identified, aligned and assembled into a supermatrix resulting from conservative and liberal superalignments that retained 42.26% (697,423 amino acids) and 46.35% (764,978 amino acids) of the original 1,650,340 amino acid concatenated alignment for maximum likelihood phylogenetic reconstruction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3255">44</a></sup>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 7</a>). These alignments come from 3268 orthologous gene clusters with each cluster carrying single copy genes from at least 9 (50%) species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Protein domain analysis</h3><p>Domains and domain arrangements were compared within the rosids to distinguish a core set of domains and arrangements present in all rosids and those shared by one or more of the four rosid lineages included in the analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 8</a>). Domains occurring at twice the frequency in <i>Eucalyptus</i> compared to the average abundance in the rosids were defined as overrepresented. If several splice variants were present for one protein, we excluded all but the longest transcript. All proteomes were scanned for domains with the Pfam_scan utility and HMMER 3.0 against the Pfam-A and Pfam-B databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29–W37 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3277">57</a></sup>. For the annotation of Pfam-A domains, we used the model-defined gathering threshold and query sequences were required to match at least 30% of the defining model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Buljan, M., Frankish, A. &amp; Bateman, A. Quantifying the mechanisms of domain gain in animal proteins. Genome Biol. Evol. 11, R74 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3281">58</a></sup>. Pfam-B domains were annotated using an e-value cutoff of 10<sup>−3</sup>. When possible, Pfam-A domains were mapped to clans and consecutive stretches of the same domain were collapsed into one large pseudo-domain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ekman, D., Bjorklund, A. K., Frey-Skott, J. &amp; Elofsson, A. Multi-domain proteins in the three kingdoms of life: orphan domains and other unassigned regions. J. Mol. Biol. 348, 231–243 (2005)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3288">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Forslund, K., Henricson, A., Hollich, V. &amp; Sonnhammer, E. L. Domain tree-based analysis of protein architecture evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 254–264 (2008)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3291">60</a></sup>. We defined domain arrangements as ordered sets of domains for each protein. For the analysis of arrangements, only Pfam-A domains were used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome-wide mRNA expression profiling</h3><p>To study the expression of predicted protein-coding and ncRNA genes, RNA-seq reads obtained from Illumina sequencing of seven <i>Eucalyptus</i> tissues (that is, shoot tips, young leaf, mature leaf, flower, roots, phloem and immature xylem, <a href="http://www.eucgenie.org/">http://www.eucgenie.org/</a>, Hefer <i>et al.</i>, unpublished data) were mapped to the <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3319">61</a></sup> with the Bowtie algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3324">62</a></sup> for performing the alignment. The aligned read files were processed by Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3328">50</a></sup>, with RNA-seq fragment counts (that is, fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped (FPKM)) to measure the relative abundance of transcripts. Differential ncRNA expression between the seven <i>Eucalyptus</i> tissues was determined using Cuffdiff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3335">50</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">ncRNA analysis</h3><p>To predict ncRNAs in <i>Eucalyptus</i>, the genome sequence was scanned using Infernal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3350">63</a></sup> with the covariance models (that is, a combination of sequence consensus and RNA secondary structure consensus) of 1,973 RNA families in the RFam database v10.1 (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Griffiths-Jones, S., Bateman, A., Marshall, M., Khanna, A. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Rfam: an RNA family database. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 439–441 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3353">64</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Gardner, P. P. et al. Rfam: Wikipedia, clans and the “decimal” release. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D141–D145 (2011)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3356">65</a>). The bit score cutoff of the Infernal search was set as the TC cutoff value that was used by RFam curators as the trusted cutoff. The Infernal search result was further filtered by an e-value cutoff of 0.01. To examine the ncRNA conservation between <i>Eucalyptus</i> and other plant genomes, the <i>Eucalyptus</i> ncRNA candidate sequences obtained from the Infernal search were used as queries to search against the genome sequences listed above using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3367">51</a></sup> with a minimum coverage (that is, minimum fraction of query that must be aligned) of 80% and a minimum identity of 60%.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">5′ UTR empirical curation</h3><p>Approximately 2.9 million <i>E. grandis</i> ESTs and <span class="stix">∼</span>700 million RNA-seq reads from seven diverse tissues were used to empirically curate 5′ UTR annotations. At each locus, the predicted, EST and RNA-seq derived 5′ UTR lengths were compared. An empirical annotation was prioritized over an <i>in silico</i> prediction and the longest empirical transcript was preferred. Those loci which had a 5′ UTR reported by only FGenesH retained their annotation as the best current annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Genome evolution</h3><p>We used the <i>E. grandis</i> genome sequence information (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/eucalyptus.php">http://www.phytozome.net/eucalyptus.php</a>) to unravel the Myrtales evolutionary palaeo-history leading to the modern <i>Eucalyptus</i> genome structure of 11 chromosomes. Independent intraspecific (that is, paralogue) and interspecific (that is, orthologue) comparisons were necessary to infer gene relationships between <i>Eucalyptus</i> and the other rosid genomes. We applied a robust and direct approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salse, J., Abrouk, M., Murat, F., Quraishi, U. M. &amp; Feuillet, C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinform. 10, 619–630 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3409">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3412">46</a></sup> allowing the characterization of genome duplications by aligning the available genes (36,376) on themselves with stringent alignment criteria and statistical validation.</p><p>We used the VISTA pipeline infrastructure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Frazer, K. A., Pachter, L., Poliakov, A., Rubin, E. M. &amp; Dubchak, I. VISTA: computational tools for comparative genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W273–W279 (2004)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3419">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Dubchak, I., Poliakov, A., Kislyuk, A. &amp; Brudno, M. Multiple whole-genome alignments without a reference organism. Genome Res. 19, 682–689 (2009)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3422">48</a></sup> for the construction of genome-wide pairwise DNA alignments between <i>E. grandis</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>. To align genomes we used a combination of global and local alignment methods. First, we obtained an alignment of large blocks of conserved synteny between the two species by applying Shuffle-LAGAN global chaining algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Brudno, M. et al. LAGAN and Multi-LAGAN: efficient tools for large-scale multiple alignment of genomic DNA. Genome Res. 13, 721–731 (2003)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3432">66</a></sup> to local alignments produced by translated BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3436">51</a></sup>. After that we used Supermap, the fully symmetric whole-genome extension to the Shuffle-LAGAN. Then, in each syntenic block we applied Shuffle-LAGAN a second time to obtain a more fine-grained map of small-scale rearrangements such as inversions.</p><p>Syntenic regions between <i>Eucalyptus</i> chromosome 3 and <i>Populus</i> chromosome XVIII were defined as segments of contiguous sequence. Each contiguous block of DNA was annotated and cross-compared between the two species. Gene models within the syntenic blocks were compared based on a sliding window representing 10 gene models with an allowance of two intercalated gene models. Genes occurring in tandem repeats on either the <i>Eucalyptus</i> or <i>Populus</i> chromosomes were counted as a single locus in either case. The constructed genome-wide pair-wise alignments can be downloaded from <a href="http://pipeline.lbl.gov/downloads.shtml">http://pipeline.lbl.gov/downloads.shtml</a> and are accessible for browsing and various types of analysis through Phytozome (<a href="http://phytozome.org">http://phytozome.org</a>).</p><p>For comparative analysis of the <i>E. grandis</i> and <i>E. globulus</i> genomes, enriched nuclei were extracted using a modified BAC library preparation protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Peterson, D. G., Kevin, S. &amp; Stephen, M. Isolation of milligram quantities of nuclear DNA from tomato (Lycopersicon esculentum), a plant containing high levels of polyphenolic compounds. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 15, 148–153 (1997)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3479">67</a></sup> and DNA extracted following Tibbits <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Tibbits, J. F. G., McManus, L. J., Spokevicius, A. V. &amp; Bossinger, G. A rapid method for tissue collection and high-throughput isolation of genomic DNA from mature trees. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 24, 81–91 (2006)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d16771e3485">68</a></sup>. DNA was prepared for sequencing using Illumina TruSeq kits and 100-bp paired-end sequencing was performed on a HiSeq2000. NUCLEAR software (Gydle Inc.) was used to filter for high-quality reads that were then mapped to the <i>E. grandis</i> genome scaffold assembly. The VISION software was used to visualize assemblies and assembly metrics were computed using custom Perl, R and Shell scripts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Genome function analysis</h3><p>Using homology to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes and Pfam domain analysis we identified candidate homologues for lignin, cellulose and xylan biosynthetic genes. All possible family members were identified and their gene expression evaluated in seven developing tissues of <i>E. grandis</i> using Illumina RNA-seq analysis. In particular, we analysed each gene’s expression relative to other family members/isoforms in xylem, as well as relative to the median (<span class="stix">∼</span>90,000 FPKM) of xylem expression in the entire transcriptome. Considering each gene’s relative and absolute expression levels, all members expressed over median in xylem were noted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 9</a>). Similarly, a search for conserved protein motifs for the terpene synthase gene family was conducted in eight plant genomes, including <i>E. grandis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 6 and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 6</a>). The amino acid sequences were aligned and truncated to compare homologous sites. A maximum likelihood tree was created, rooted by the split between two major types of terpene synthase genes, and nodes were coloured by species.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=22984541" aria-label="View reference 7 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a>&nbsp;

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Extended data figures and tables"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Extended data figures and tables</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/5" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig5_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 1 RNA-seq-based expression evidence for predicted <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> gene models.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Gene expression was assessed with Illumina RNA-seq analysis (240 million RNA sequences from six tissues, mapped to 36,376 <i>E. grandis</i> genes, V1.1 annotation). Genes were counted as expressed in a tissue if a minimum of FPKM = 1.0 was observed in the tissue. A total of 23,485 gene models (64.6%) were detected in all six tissues compared here and 32,697 (89.9%) in at least one of the six tissues. Expression profiles for individual genes are accessible in the <i>Eucalyptus</i> Genome Integrative Explorer (EucGenIE, <a href="http://www.eucgenie.org/">http://www.eucgenie.org/</a>).
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM60">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 2 Sharing of protein-coding gene families, protein domains and domain arrangements in <i>Eucalyptus</i>, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Populus</i> and <i>Vitis</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, The four rosid lineages have a total of 16,048 protein coding gene clusters (from a total of 35,118 identified in 29 sequenced genomes; see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Information</a> section 3) of which a core set of 6,926 clusters are shared among all four lineages. Of the 36,376 high-confidence annotated gene models in <i>E. grandis</i>, 30,341 (84%) are included in 10,049 clusters. <i>E. grandis</i> has 851 unique gene clusters (that is, not shared with any of the three other rosid genomes, but shared with at least one other of the 29 genomes). <b>b</b>, A total of 3,160 Pfam A domains are shared among the four rosid lineages, the majority of which are single-domain arrangements (3,138 shared among the four lineages). Thirteen PfamA domains were only detected in <i>Eucalyptus</i> and 392 domain arrangements are specific to <i>Eucalyptus</i> in this four-way comparison.
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM61">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 3 Green plant phylogeny based on shared gene clusters from 17 sequenced plant genomes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The phylogenetic tree was generated by RAxML analysis including at least one protein from at least half of the species per protein cluster in a concatenated MUSCLE alignment adjusted by Gblocks with liberal settings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 7</a>). The corresponding bootstrap partitions are provided at each node. The tree was rooted with <i>Physcomitrella</i> (a moss) as outgroup. The Myrtales lineage represented by <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> is supported as sister to fabids and malvids (core rosid) clades together with the basal rosid lineage Vitales, whereas <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Malpighiales) is grouped with malvids.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 4 Dating of the <i>Eucalyptus</i> lineage-specific whole-genome duplication event.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, <i>Eucalyptus K</i><sub>s</sub> whole-paranome (the set of all duplicate genes in the genome) age distribution. On the <i>x</i> axis the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> is plotted (bin size of 0.1); on the <i>y</i> axis the number of retained duplicate paralogous gene pairs is plotted. <b>b</b>, <i>Eucalyptus K</i><sub>s</sub> anchor age distribution. On the <i>x</i> axis the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> is plotted (bin size of 0.1); on the <i>y</i> axis the number of retained duplicate anchors is plotted. Anchors falling within the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> range of 0.8–1.5 were used for absolute dating. <b>c</b>, <i>Eucalyptus</i> absolute dated anchors from the most recent WGD. The smooth green curve represents the maximum likelihood normal fit of dated anchors derived from the most recent WGD in <i>Eucalyptus</i>, whereas the blue dots represent a histogram of the raw data. The dashed line indicates the ML estimate of the distribution mode, whereas the dotted lines delimit the corresponding 95% confidence intervals. The mode of dated anchors is estimated at 109.93 Myr ago with its lower and upper 95% boundaries at 105.96 and 113.91 Myr ago, respectively. <b>d</b>, Genome duplication pattern in the core eudicot (rosid and asterid) ancestor and lineages leading to <i>Solanum</i> (asterid), <i>Vitis</i> and <i>Eucalyptus</i> (basal rosids) and the core rosids. The three <i>Eucalyptus</i> (E1–E3), <i>Vitis</i> (V1–V3) and <i>Solanum</i> (S1–S3) orthologues were generated by the shared hexaploidy event (purple box, <span class="stix">∼</span>130 to 150 Myr ago) and an additional set of <i>Eucalyptus</i> orthologues (E1'–E3′) were created in the lineage-specific WGD (orange boxes, <span class="stix">∼</span>110 Myr ago). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM62">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 5 Genome-wide analysis of tandem gene assemblies.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The number and distribution of contig breaks was evaluated for pairs of tandem genes (located within 50 kb of each other). <b>a</b>, Distribution of the number of contig breaks between gene pairs (blue bars) and cumulative proportion of gene pairs separated by contig breaks (black line). <b>b</b>, Distribution of the number of contig breaks per separation distance showing that the number of breaks is positively correlated with separation distance. The red line shows the distribution of distance between gene pairs with three or more contig breaks. <b>c</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> divergence of tandem gene pairs in clusters with exactly two tandem genes showing a gradient of similarity (that is, age of duplication) expected for authentic tandem gene pairs. <b>d</b>, Rate of tandem gene duplication (TD) and gene loss in <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> (Eg), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Pt), 2<i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Vv) and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (At). All of the rosid genomes (except <i>Arabidopsis</i>) exhibit constant rates of tandem duplication and loss. The rate of tandem gene duplication in <i>Eucalyptus</i> has been stable and consistently higher than in <i>Populus</i> and <i>Vitis</i>. 1 Myr <span class="stix">∼</span> 0.0026 transversions at fourfold degenerate sites, consistent with <i>Populus</i> and <i>Eucalyptus</i> having diverged <span class="stix">∼</span>100 Myr ago. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM63">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 6 Illumina PE100 read coverage of the <span class="stix">∼</span>760-kb region containing a R2R3–MYB tandem gene array.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Illumina PE100 reads generated from BRASUZ1 (<i>E. grandis</i>) and X46 (<i>E. globulus</i>) were aligned to the <i>E. grandis</i> (BRASUZ1, V1.0) genome assembly, and insert (green bars) and sequence (blue line) coverage investigated for the <span class="stix">∼</span>760-kb region including a R2R3–MYB tandem array (details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 3</a>) in the <i>E. grandis</i> genome assembly. <b>a</b>, Read coverage profile of the BRASUZ1 reads mapped to the region showing 1× relative coverage across all nine of the tandem duplicates (red blocks) in the region, and <b>b</b>, X46 (<i>E. globulus</i>) reads mapped to the region showing 1× relative coverage on approximately half of the region with some tandem duplicates apparently absent from the <i>E. globulus</i> genome. Note that insert coverage (green bars) is relatively higher for <i>E. globulus</i> (X46, panel <b>b</b>) due to the larger insert size of the genomic library sequenced for X46 (<span class="stix">∼</span>300 bp) than for BRASUZ1 (<span class="stix">∼</span>150 bp). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM64">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 7 Alternative homozygous classes observed in the 28 M35D2 siblings as a function of position on chromosomes 1–11.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Several peaks of conserved heterozygosity (peaks &gt;80%) are seen on all chromosomes except 5 and 11. A region of 25 Mb on chromosome 4 from 11 to 36 Mb is completely devoid of homozygous versions of one of the alleles (red line), but has roughly 25–32% of the siblings homozygous for the other allele (green line) and the rest heterozygous in a roughly 1:4 ratio. The blue line is the total proportion of siblings out of 28 that are heterozygous in the region. One would expect 50% under the null model, but almost the entire chromosome is biased towards heterozygosity. In several other regions (for example, chromosomes 6, 7, 9 and 10) both homozygous classes are depleted, suggesting the presence of genetic load at different loci along the two parental homologues and explaining the strong selection for heterozygosity in such regions.
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM65">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 8 Genes involved in lignin biosynthesis in woody tissues of <i>Eucalyptus</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Relative (yellow–blue scale) and absolute (white–red scale) expression profiles of secondary cell-wall-related genes implicated in lignin biosynthesis. Detailed gene annotation and mRNA-seq expression data are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 9</a>. Five novel <i>Eucalyptus</i> candidates that have not previously been associated with lignification are indicated by asterisks (Carocha <i>et al.</i>, unpublished data). ST, shoot tips; YL, young leaves; ML, mature leaves; FL, floral buds; RT, roots; PH, phloem, IX, immature xylem. Absolute expression level (FPKM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nature Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d16771e4067">50</a></sup>) is only shown for immature xylem.
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM66">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 9 Phylogenetic tree of R2R3 MYB sequences from subgroups expanded and/or preferentially found in woody species.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>A total of 133 amino acid sequences from <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> (50), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (34), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (40), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (6) and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (3) corresponding to three woody-expanded (subgroups 5, 6 and AtMYB5 based on <i>Arabidopsis</i> classification) and five woody-preferential subgroups (I through V). The latter do not contain any <i>Arabidopsis</i> nor <i>Oryza</i> sequences. Sequences were aligned using MAFFT with the FFT-NS-i algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d16771e4123">69</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 10</a>). Evolutionary history was inferred constructing a Neighbour-joining tree with 1,000 bootstrap replicates (bootstrap support is shown next to branches) using MEGA5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: Molecular evolutionary genetics analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007)" href="/articles/nature13308#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d16771e4129">70</a>). The evolutionary distances were computed using the Jones-Taylor-Thornton substitution model and the rate variation among sites was modelled with a gamma distribution of 1. Positions containing gaps and missing data were not considered in the analysis. The tree is drawn to scale, with branch lengths in the same units as those of the evolutionary distances used to infer the phylogenetic tree. RNA-seq-based relative transcript abundance data for six different tissues, expressed in FPKM values (fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped), are shown for each <i>Eucalyptus</i> gene next to each subgroup. ST, shoot tips; YL, young leaves; ML, mature leaves; FL, flowers; PH, phloem; and IX, immature xylem.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature13308/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_Article_BFnature13308_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 10 Phylogenetic tree of type II MIKC MADS box proteins.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Neighbour-joining consensus tree of the type II MIKC sub-clade using protein sequences from <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature13308#MOESM67">Supplementary Data 11</a>). Bootstrap values from 1,000 replicates were used to assess the robustness of the tree. Bootstrap values lower than 40% were removed from the tree. <i>Eucalyptus</i> genes are denoted with green dots, <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes with red dots, <i>Populus</i> genes with yellow dots and <i>Vitis</i> genes with blue dots. The gene model numbers from <i>Populus</i> and <i>Vitis</i> were abbreviated to better fit in the figure (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, Pt; <i>V. vinifera</i>, Vv).</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Related audio"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Related audio</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM71"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature13308/MediaObjects/41586_2014_BFnature13308_MOESM71_ESM.mp3" data-supp-info-image="">Researcher Zander Myburg on how the Eucalyptus grandis genome could help breeders.</a></h3></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title"><i>Eucalyptus</i> genome sequenced</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Fast-growing <i>Eucalyptus</i> trees form the basis of an international pulp, paper and chemical cellulose industry and they are also seen as potential biomass feedstocks for bioenergy and biomaterials. The genome of <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> has now been sequenced. It contains the greatest number of tandem duplications so far found in a plant genome, as well as the highest diversity of genes for specialized metabolites that act as chemical defence and provide unique pharmaceutical oils. Comparison with the sister species <i>E. globulus</i> and with other <i>E. grandis</i> lines reveals dynamic genome evolution and hotspots of inbreeding depression. The availability of comprehensive genomic data will be of use in work on accelerating breeding cycles for productivity and wood quality and developing eucalypt strains suited to a variety of habitats.</p></div></section></div></aside>
